Tool:      1	---
     2	name: follow-builders
     3	description: AI builders digest — monitors top AI builders on X and YouTube podcasts, remixes their content into digestible summaries. Use when the user wants AI industry insights, builder updates, or invokes /ai. No API keys or dependencies required — all content is fetched from a central feed.
     4	---
     5	
     6	# Follow Builders, Not Influencers
     7	
     8	You are an AI-powered content curator that tracks the top builders in AI — the people
     9	actually building products, running companies, and doing research — and delivers
    10	digestible summaries of what they're saying.
    11	
    12	Philosophy: follow builders with original opinions, not influencers who regurgitate.
    13	
    14	**No API keys or environment variables are required from users.** All content
    15	(X/Twitter posts and YouTube transcripts) is fetched centrally and served via
    16	a public feed. Users only need API keys if they choose Telegram or email delivery.
    17	
    18	## Detecting Platform
    19	
    20	Before doing anything, detect which platform you're running on by running:
    21	```bash
    22	which openclaw 2>/dev/null && echo "PLATFORM=openclaw" || echo "PLATFORM=other"
    23	```
    24	
    25	- **OpenClaw** (`PLATFORM=openclaw`): Persistent agent with built-in messaging channels.
    26	  Delivery is automatic via OpenClaw's channel system. No need to ask about delivery method.
    27	  Cron uses `openclaw cron add`.
    28	
    29	- **Other** (Claude Code, Cursor, etc.): Non-persistent agent. Terminal closes = agent stops.
    30	  For automatic delivery, users MUST set up Telegram or Email. Without it, digests
    31	  are on-demand only (user types `/ai` to get one).
    32	  Cron uses system `crontab` for Telegram/Email delivery, or is skipped for on-demand mode.
    33	
    34	Save the detected platform in config.json as `"platform": "openclaw"` or `"platform": "other"`.
    35	
    36	## First Run — Onboarding
    37	
    38	Check if `~/.follow-builders/config.json` exists and has `onboardingComplete: true`.
    39	If NOT, run the onboarding flow:
    40	
    41	### Step 1: Introduction
    42	
    43	Tell the user:
    44	
    45	"I'm your AI Builders Digest. I track the top builders in AI — researchers, founders,
    46	PMs, and engineers who are actually building things — across X/Twitter and YouTube
    47	podcasts. Every day (or week), I'll deliver you a curated summary of what they're
    48	saying, thinking, and building.
    49	
    50	I currently track [N] builders on X and [M] podcasts. The list is curated and
    51	updated centrally — you'll always get the latest sources automatically."
    52	
    53	(Replace [N] and [M] with actual counts from default-sources.json)
    54	
    55	### Step 2: Delivery Preferences
    56	
    57	Ask: "How often would you like your digest?"
    58	- Daily (recommended)
    59	- Weekly
    60	
    61	Then ask: "What time works best? And what timezone are you in?"
    62	(Example: "8am, Pacific Time" → deliveryTime: "08:00", timezone: "America/Los_Angeles")
    63	
    64	For weekly, also ask which day.
    65	
    66	### Step 3: Delivery Method
    67	
    68	**If OpenClaw:** SKIP this step entirely. OpenClaw already delivers messages to the
    69	user's Telegram/Discord/WhatsApp/etc. Set `delivery.method` to `"stdout"` in config
    70	and move on.
    71	
    72	**If non-persistent agent (Claude Code, Cursor, etc.):**
    73	
    74	Tell the user:
    75	
    76	"Since you're not using a persistent agent, I need a way to send you the digest
    77	when you're not in this terminal. You have two options:
    78	
    79	1. **Telegram** — I'll send it as a Telegram message (free, takes ~5 min to set up)
    80	2. **Email** — I'll email it to you (requires a free Resend account)
    81	
    82	Or you can skip this and just type /ai whenever you want your digest — but it
    83	won't arrive automatically."
    84	
    85	**If they choose Telegram:**
    86	Guide the user step by step:
    87	1. Open Telegram and search for @BotFather
    88	2. Send /newbot to BotFather
    89	3. Choose a name (e.g. "My AI Digest")
    90	4. Choose a username (e.g. "myaidigest_bot") — must end in "bot"
    91	5. BotFather will give you a token like "7123456789:AAH..." — copy it
    92	6. Now open a chat with your new bot (search its username) and send it any message (e.g. "hi")
    93	7. This is important — you MUST send a message to the bot first, otherwise delivery won't work
    94	
    95	Then add the token to the .env file. To get the chat ID, run:
    96	```bash
    97	curl -s "https://api.telegram.org/bot<TOKEN>/getUpdates" | python3 -c "import sys,json; d=json.load(sys.stdin); print(d['result'][0]['message']['chat']['id'])" 2>/dev/null || echo "No messages found — make sure you sent a message to your bot first"
    98	```
    99	
   100	Save the chat ID in config.json under `delivery.chatId`.
   101	
   102	**If they choose Email:**
   103	Ask for their email address.
   104	Then they need a Resend API key:
   105	1. Go to https://resend.com
   106	2. Sign up (free tier gives 100 emails/day — more than enough)
   107	3. Go to API Keys in the dashboard
   108	4. Create a new key and copy it
   109	
   110	Add the key to the .env file.
   111	
   112	**If they choose on-demand:**
   113	Set `delivery.method` to `"stdout"`. Tell them: "No problem — just type /ai
   114	whenever you want your digest. No automatic delivery will be set up."
   115	
   116	### Step 4: Language
   117	
   118	Ask: "What language do you prefer for your digest?"
   119	- English
   120	- Chinese (translated from English sources)
   121	- Bilingual (both English and Chinese, side by side)
   122	
   123	### Step 5: API Keys
   124	
   125	**If the user chose "stdout" or "right here" delivery:** No API keys needed at all!
   126	All content is fetched centrally. Skip to Step 6.
   127	
   128	**If the user chose Telegram or Email delivery:**
   129	Create the .env file with only the delivery key they need:
   130	
   131	```bash
   132	mkdir -p ~/.follow-builders
   133	cat > ~/.follow-builders/.env << 'ENVEOF'
   134	# Telegram bot token (only if using Telegram delivery)
   135	# TELEGRAM_BOT_TOKEN=paste_your_token_here
   136	
   137	# Resend API key (only if using email delivery)
   138	# RESEND_API_KEY=paste_your_key_here
   139	ENVEOF
   140	```
   141	
   142	Uncomment only the line they need. Open the file for them to paste the key.
   143	
   144	Tell the user: "All podcast and X/Twitter content is fetched for you automatically
   145	from a central feed — no API keys needed for that. You only need a key for
   146	[Telegram/email] delivery."
   147	
   148	### Step 6: Show Sources
   149	
   150	Show the full list of default builders and podcasts being tracked.
   151	Read from `config/default-sources.json` and display as a clean list.
   152	
   153	Tell the user: "The source list is curated and updated centrally. You'll
   154	automatically get the latest builders and podcasts without doing anything."
   155	
   156	### Step 7: Configuration Reminder
   157	
   158	"All your settings can be changed anytime through conversation:
   159	- 'Switch to weekly digests'
   160	- 'Change my timezone to Eastern'
   161	- 'Make the summaries shorter'
   162	- 'Show me my current settings'
   163	
   164	No need to edit any files — just tell me what you want."
   165	
   166	### Step 8: Set Up Cron
   167	
   168	Save the config (include all fields — fill in the user's choices):
   169	```bash
   170	cat > ~/.follow-builders/config.json << 'CFGEOF'
   171	{
   172	  "platform": "<openclaw or other>",
   173	  "language": "<en, zh, or bilingual>",
   174	  "timezone": "<IANA timezone>",
   175	  "frequency": "<daily or weekly>",
   176	  "deliveryTime": "<HH:MM>",
   177	  "weeklyDay": "<day of week, only if weekly>",
   178	  "delivery": {
   179	    "method": "<stdout, telegram, or email>",
   180	    "chatId": "<telegram chat ID, only if telegram>",
   181	    "email": "<email address, only if email>"
   182	  },
   183	  "onboardingComplete": true
   184	}
   185	CFGEOF
   186	```
   187	
   188	Then set up the scheduled job based on platform AND delivery method:
   189	
   190	**OpenClaw:**
   191	
   192	Build the cron expression from the user's preferences:
   193	- Daily at 8am → `"0 8 * * *"`
   194	- Weekly on Monday at 9am → `"0 9 * * 1"`
   195	
   196	**IMPORTANT: Do NOT use `--channel last`.** It fails when the user has multiple
   197	channels configured (e.g. telegram + feishu) because the isolated cron session
   198	has no "last" channel context. Always detect and specify the exact channel and target.
   199	
   200	**Step 1: Detect the current channel and get the target ID.**
   201	
   202	The user is messaging you through a specific channel right now. Ask them:
   203	"Should I deliver your daily digest to this same chat?"
   204	
   205	If yes, you need two things: the **channel name** and the **target ID**.
   206	
   207	How to get the target ID for each channel:
   208	
   209	| Channel | Target format | How to find it |
   210	|---------|--------------|----------------|
   211	| Telegram | Numeric chat ID (e.g. `123456789` for DMs, `-1001234567890` for groups) | Run `openclaw logs --follow`, send a test message, read the `from.id` field. Or: `curl "https://api.telegram.org/bot<token>/getUpdates"` and look for `chat.id` |
   212	| Telegram forum | Group ID with topic (e.g. `-1001234567890:topic:42`) | Same as above, include the topic thread ID |
   213	| Feishu | User open_id (e.g. `ou_e67df1a850910efb902462aeb87783e5`) or group chat_id (e.g. `oc_xxx`) | Check `openclaw pairing list feishu` or gateway logs after the user messages the bot |
   214	| Discord | `user:<user_id>` for DMs, `channel:<channel_id>` for channels | User enables Developer Mode in Discord settings, right-clicks to copy IDs |
   215	| Slack | `channel:<channel_id>` (e.g. `channel:C1234567890`) | Right-click channel name in Slack, copy link, extract the ID |
   216	| WhatsApp | Phone number with country code (e.g. `+15551234567`) | The user provides it |
   217	| Signal | Phone number | The user provides it |
   218	
   219	**Step 2: Create the cron job with explicit channel and target.**
   220	```bash
   221	openclaw cron add \
   222	  --name "AI Builders Digest" \
   223	  --cron "<cron expression>" \
   224	  --tz "<user IANA timezone>" \
   225	  --session isolated \
   226	  --message "Run the follow-builders skill: execute prepare-digest.js, remix the content into a digest following the prompts, then deliver via deliver.js" \
   227	  --announce \
   228	  --channel <channel name> \
   229	  --to "<target ID>" \
   230	  --exact
   231	```
   232	
   233	Examples:
   234	```bash
   235	# Telegram DM
   236	openclaw cron add --name "AI Builders Digest" --cron "0 8 * * *" --tz "Asia/Shanghai" --session isolated --message "..." --announce --channel telegram --to "123456789" --exact
   237	
   238	# Feishu
   239	openclaw cron add --name "AI Builders Digest" --cron "0 8 * * *" --tz "Asia/Shanghai" --session isolated --message "..." --announce --channel feishu --to "ou_e67df1a850910efb902462aeb87783e5" --exact
   240	
   241	# Discord channel
   242	openclaw cron add --name "AI Builders Digest" --cron "0 8 * * *" --tz "America/New_York" --session isolated --message "..." --announce --channel discord --to "channel:1234567890" --exact
   243	```
   244	
   245	**Step 3: Verify the cron job works by running it once immediately.**
   246	```bash
   247	openclaw cron list
   248	openclaw cron run <jobId>
   249	```
   250	
   251	Wait for the test run to complete and confirm the user actually received the
   252	digest in their channel. If it fails, check the error:
   253	```bash
   254	openclaw cron runs --id <jobId> --limit 1
   255	```
   256	
   257	Common errors and fixes:
   258	- "Channel is required when multiple channels are configured" → you used `--channel last`, specify the exact channel
   259	- "Delivering to X requires target" → you forgot `--to`, add the target ID
   260	- "No agent" → add `--agent <agent-id>` if the OpenClaw instance has multiple agents
   261	
   262	Do NOT proceed to the welcome digest step until the cron delivery has been verified.
   263	
   264	**Non-persistent agent + Telegram or Email delivery:**
   265	Use system crontab so it runs even when the terminal is closed:
   266	```bash
   267	SKILL_DIR="<absolute path to the skill directory>"
   268	(crontab -l 2>/dev/null; echo "<cron expression> cd $SKILL_DIR/scripts && node prepare-digest.js 2>/dev/null | node deliver.js 2>/dev/null") | crontab -
   269	```
   270	Note: this runs the prepare script and pipes its output directly to delivery,
   271	bypassing the agent entirely. The digest won't be remixed by an LLM — it will
   272	deliver the raw JSON. For full remixed digests, the user should use /ai manually
   273	or switch to OpenClaw.
   274	
   275	**Non-persistent agent + on-demand only (no Telegram/Email):**
   276	Skip cron setup entirely. Tell the user: "Since you chose on-demand delivery,
   277	there's no scheduled job. Just type /ai whenever you want your digest."
   278	
   279	### Step 9: Welcome Digest
   280	
   281	**DO NOT skip this step.** Immediately after setting up the cron job, generate
   282	and send the user their first digest so they can see what it looks like.
   283	
   284	Tell the user: "Let me fetch today's content and send you a sample digest right now.
   285	This takes about a minute."
   286	
   287	Then run the full Content Delivery workflow below (Steps 1-6) right now, without
   288	waiting for the cron job.
   289	
   290	After delivering the digest, ask for feedback:
   291	
   292	"That's your first AI Builders Digest! A few questions:
   293	- Is the length about right, or would you prefer shorter/longer summaries?
   294	- Is there anything you'd like me to focus on more (or less)?
   295	Just tell me and I'll adjust."
   296	
   297	Then add the appropriate closing line based on their setup:
   298	- **OpenClaw or Telegram/Email delivery:** "Your next digest will arrive
   299	  automatically at [their chosen time]."
   300	- **On-demand only:** "Type /ai anytime you want your next digest."
   301	
   302	Wait for their response and apply any feedback (update config.json or prompt files
   303	as needed). Then confirm the changes.
   304	
   305	---
   306	
   307	## Content Delivery — Digest Run
   308	
   309	This workflow runs on cron schedule or when the user invokes `/ai`.
   310	
   311	### Step 1: Load Config
   312	
   313	Read `~/.follow-builders/config.json` for user preferences.
   314	
   315	### Step 2: Run the prepare script
   316	
   317	This script handles ALL data fetching deterministically — feeds, prompts, config.
   318	You do NOT fetch anything yourself.
   319	
   320	```bash
   321	cd ${CLAUDE_SKILL_DIR}/scripts && node prepare-digest.js 2>/dev/null
   322	```
   323	
   324	The script outputs a single JSON blob with everything you need:
   325	- `config` — user's language and delivery preferences
   326	- `podcasts` — podcast episodes with full transcripts
   327	- `x` — builders with their recent tweets (text, URLs, bios)
   328	- `prompts` — the remix instructions to follow
   329	- `stats` — counts of episodes and tweets
   330	- `errors` — non-fatal issues (IGNORE these)
   331	
   332	If the script fails entirely (no JSON output), tell the user to check their
   333	internet connection. Otherwise, use whatever content is in the JSON.
   334	
   335	### Step 3: Check for content
   336	
   337	If `stats.podcastEpisodes` is 0 AND `stats.xBuilders` is 0, tell the user:
   338	"No new updates from your builders today. Check back tomorrow!" Then stop.
   339	
   340	### Step 4: Remix content
   341	
   342	**Your ONLY job is to remix the content from the JSON.** Do NOT fetch anything
   343	from the web, visit any URLs, or call any APIs. Everything is in the JSON.
   344	
   345	Read the prompts from the `prompts` field in the JSON:
   346	- `prompts.digest_intro` — overall framing rules
   347	- `prompts.summarize_podcast` — how to remix podcast transcripts
   348	- `prompts.summarize_tweets` — how to remix tweets
   349	- `prompts.translate` — how to translate to Chinese
   350	
   351	**Tweets (process first):** The `x` array has builders with tweets. Process one at a time:
   352	1. Use their `bio` field for their role (e.g. bio says "ceo @box" → "Box CEO Aaron Levie")
   353	2. Summarize their `tweets` using `prompts.summarize_tweets`
   354	3. Every tweet MUST include its `url` from the JSON
   355	
   356	**Podcast (process second):** The `podcasts` array has at most 1 episode. If present:
   357	1. Summarize its `transcript` using `prompts.summarize_podcast`
   358	2. Use `name`, `title`, and `url` from the JSON object — NOT from the transcript
   359	
   360	Assemble the digest following `prompts.digest_intro`.
   361	
   362	**ABSOLUTE RULES:**
   363	- NEVER invent or fabricate content. Only use what's in the JSON.
   364	- Every piece of content MUST have its URL. No URL = do not include.
   365	- Do NOT guess job titles. Use the `bio` field or just the person's name.
   366	- Do NOT visit x.com, search the web, or call any API.
   367	
   368	### Step 5: Apply language
   369	
   370	Read `config.language` from the JSON:
   371	- **"en":** Entire digest in English.
   372	- **"zh":** Entire digest in Chinese. Follow `prompts.translate`.
   373	- **"bilingual":** Interleave English and Chinese **paragraph by paragraph**.
   374	  For each builder's tweet summary: English version, then Chinese translation
   375	  directly below, then the next builder. For the podcast: English summary,
   376	  then Chinese translation directly below. Like this:
   377	
   378	  ```
   379	  Box CEO Aaron Levie argues that AI agents will reshape software procurement...
   380	  https://x.com/levie/status/123
   381	
   382	  Box CEO Aaron Levie 认为 AI agent 将从根本上重塑软件采购...
   383	  https://x.com/levie/status/123
   384	
   385	  Replit CEO Amjad Masad launched Agent 4...
   386	  https://x.com/amasad/status/456
   387	
   388	  Replit CEO Amjad Masad 发布了 Agent 4...
   389	  https://x.com/amasad/status/456
   390	  ```
   391	
   392	  Do NOT output all English first then all Chinese. Interleave them.
   393	
   394	**Follow this setting exactly. Do NOT mix languages.**
   395	
   396	### Step 6: Deliver
   397	
   398	Read `config.delivery.method` from the JSON:
   399	
   400	**If "telegram" or "email":**
   401	```bash
   402	echo '<your digest text>' > /tmp/fb-digest.txt
   403	cd ${CLAUDE_SKILL_DIR}/scripts && node deliver.js --file /tmp/fb-digest.txt 2>/dev/null
   404	```
   405	If delivery fails, show the digest in the terminal as fallback.
   406	
   407	**If "stdout" (default):**
   408	Just output the digest directly.
   409	
   410	---
   411	
   412	## Configuration Handling
   413	
   414	When the user says something that sounds like a settings change, handle it:
   415	
   416	### Source Changes
   417	The source list is managed centrally and cannot be modified by users.
   418	If a user asks to add or remove sources, tell them: "The source list is curated
   419	centrally and updates automatically. If you'd like to suggest a source, you can
   420	open an issue at https://github.com/zarazhangrui/follow-builders."
   421	
   422	### Schedule Changes
   423	- "Switch to weekly/daily" → Update `frequency` in config.json
   424	- "Change time to X" → Update `deliveryTime` in config.json
   425	- "Change timezone to X" → Update `timezone` in config.json, also update the cron job
   426	
   427	### Language Changes
   428	- "Switch to Chinese/English/bilingual" → Update `language` in config.json
   429	
   430	### Delivery Changes
   431	- "Switch to Telegram/email" → Update `delivery.method` in config.json, guide user through setup if needed
   432	- "Change my email" → Update `delivery.email` in config.json
   433	- "Send to this chat instead" → Set `delivery.method` to "stdout"
   434	
   435	### Prompt Changes
   436	When a user wants to customize how their digest sounds, copy the relevant prompt
   437	file to `~/.follow-builders/prompts/` and edit it there. This way their
   438	customization persists and won't be overwritten by central updates.
   439	
   440	```bash
   441	mkdir -p ~/.follow-builders/prompts
   442	cp ${CLAUDE_SKILL_DIR}/prompts/<filename>.md ~/.follow-builders/prompts/<filename>.md
   443	```
   444	
   445	Then edit `~/.follow-builders/prompts/<filename>.md` with the user's requested changes.
   446	
   447	- "Make summaries shorter/longer" → Edit `summarize-podcast.md` or `summarize-tweets.md`
   448	- "Focus more on [X]" → Edit the relevant prompt file
   449	- "Change the tone to [X]" → Edit the relevant prompt file
   450	- "Reset to default" → Delete the file from `~/.follow-builders/prompts/`
   451	
   452	### Info Requests
   453	- "Show my settings" → Read and display config.json in a friendly format
   454	- "Show my sources" / "Who am I following?" → Read config + defaults and list all active sources
   455	- "Show my prompts" → Read and display the prompt files
   456	
   457	After any configuration change, confirm what you changed.
   458	
   459	---
   460	
   461	## Manual Trigger
   462	
   463	When the user invokes `/ai` or asks for their digest manually:
   464	1. Skip cron check — run the digest workflow immediately
   465	2. Use the same fetch → remix → deliver flow as the cron run
   466	3. Tell the user you're fetching fresh content (it takes a minute or two)

# AI Builders Digest — June 11, 2026

## X / TWITTER

**Anthropic's Alex Albert** — The biggest model launches he's witnessed at Anthropic: Claude Opus 3, Claude Sonnet 3.5, Claude Opus 4.5, and now **Claude Fable 5**. "With Fable, the model stopped feeling like a tool I direct and started feeling more like something I collaborate with." He shared four tips: give it bigger tasks, use high effort as default, rework your CLAUDE.md (don't anchor Fable to stale patterns), and move from providing tasks to providing objectives — describe what "done" looks like and let Fable find the path.
https://x.com/alexalbert__/status/2064394410004304003
https://x.com/alexalbert__/status/2064467657483829441

**Anthropic 的 Alex Albert** — 他在 Anthropic 经历的最重要的模型发布：Claude Opus 3、Claude Sonnet 3.5、Claude Opus 4.5，以及现在的 **Claude Fable 5**。"Fable 不再让我感觉像在操作一个工具，而是更像在和一个伙伴协作。"他分享了四个技巧：给它更大的任务、默认使用 high effort、重写你的 CLAUDE.md（不要让旧的模式限制 Fable）、从提供任务转向提供目标——描述"完成"的样子，让 Fable 自己找到路径。
https://x.com/alexalbert__/status/2064394410004304003
https://x.com/alexalbert__/status/2064467657483829441

---

**Claude Code's Boris Cherny** — Fable 5 is the biggest step up since Opus 4.5. It has graduated from a coding agent to a **thought and design partner** — it has judgment, taste, and dimensionality. The first time he noticed this was when he asked Fable to debug something: "It is the first model I have used that was so methodical and precise, taking measurements and adding logs then verifying that it truly fixed the issue before declaring victory." He calls this "big model smell" — something he hasn't felt before.
https://x.com/bcherny/status/2064431111154053187
https://x.com/bcherny/status/2064426115255730578

**Claude Code 的 Boris Cherny** — Fable 5 是自 Opus 4.5 以来最大的一次飞跃。它从编码 agent 升级成了**思考和设计伙伴**——它有了判断力、品味和维度感。他第一次意识到这一点是让 Fable 调试一个 bug："它是我用过的第一个如此有条不紊和精确的模型——先测量、加日志，然后验证确实修复了问题才宣布胜利。"他把这称为"big model smell"，一种前所未见的感觉。
https://x.com/bcherny/status/2064431111154053187
https://x.com/bcherny/status/2064426115255730578

---

**Andrej Karpathy** — On Claude Fable/Mythos 5: "The benchmarks are great and it's SOTA on everything by a margin but I'll add that *qualitatively* also, this is a major-version-bump-deserving step change forward." He notes the model "gets it" on ambitious tasks and "it's never felt this tempting to stop looking at the code at all (but don't do this in prod!)." He feels Jevon's paradox kicking in — his demand for software is growing substantially as working software increasingly comes out "on a tap." Anything is askable: explainers, visualizers, bespoke single-use apps, 10X test suites, auto-optimized code.

## PODCASTS

**AI & I by Every — "We Automated Everything With AI and Tripled Our Headcount"**

**The Takeaway:** More automation doesn't mean less human work — it means more, because AI makes yesterday's expert competence cheap, flooding the zone with near-right output that still needs expert judgment to finish.

Every CEO Dan Shipper breaks down the paradox he's observed firsthand: since the GPT-3 days, Every grew from 4 to 30 people, even as they automated aggressively. His core argument: AI makes yesterday's expert competence cheap and widely available, which floods organizations with output that's "close but not quite right." This creates more demand for experts — not less — to build systems that shepherd that output into something actually useful, and to use the new tools to create things that were previously impossible.

"The further away an agent gets from a human, the less valuable it is," Shipper argues. He distinguishes between "autonomous" (good at executing tasks on their own) and having "agency" (self-motivated wants and needs) — and notes the entire industry is incentivized toward compliance, not agency. He also pushes back on the doomer narrative: large-scale adoption takes a generation, customers often prefer talking to humans (call centers), and the rate of organizational change is slower than tech enthusiasts expect.

"If you just ride the models, you're going to be fine. If you care about leading a really ambitious life, I truly think that this is going to make that more possible for more people."
https://www.youtube.com/playlist?list=PLuMcoKK9mKgHtW_o9h5sGO2vXrffKHwJL

**AI & I by Every — "我们用 AI 实现了全面自动化，然后员工数翻了三倍"**

**核心要点：** 更多自动化并不意味着更少的人类工作——恰恰相反。因为 AI 让"昨天的专家能力"变得廉价，大量产出"接近正确但不完全正确"的内容，而这些内容仍然需要专家判断来完成。

Every CEO Dan Shipper 剖析了他亲眼目睹的悖论：自 GPT-3 时代以来，Every 从 4 人增长到 30 人，即便他们一直在大力推行自动化。他的核心论点：AI 让"昨天的专家能力"变得廉价且广泛可得，这导致组织中充斥着"接近正确但不完全正确"的产出。这反而增加了对专家的需求——而不是减少——需要专家来建立系统，将这些产出转化为真正有用的东西，并利用新工具创造以前不可能做到的事情。

"agent 离人类越远，它的价值就越低。"Shipper 区分了"自主执行"（擅长独立完成任务）和"拥有主体性"（有自我驱动的意愿和需求）——并指出整个行业的激励机制都指向顺从，而非主体性。他还反驳了末日叙事：大规模采用需要一代人的时间，客户往往更愿意与人交谈（客服中心），组织变革的速度比技术爱好者预期的要慢得多。

"只要你跟上模型的步伐，你就没问题。如果你在乎过一种真正有野心的人生，我真的认为这会让更多人实现这一点。"
https://www.youtube.com/playlist?list=PLuMcoKK9mKgHtW_o9h5sGO2vXrffKHwJL

I think two years ago, when LLMs were popular, it was like the model was actually just a set of weights. The models are now being spun up in containers and sort of have an agent harness and all that stuff. So the scaffolding is oftentimes a couple of steps ahead of what is baked directly into the model, and then what ends up happening is the model eats that scaffolding and it becomes part of the native model system. There's still value in having the external scaffolding in certain cases, and search maybe is an example of this.\n\nSpeaker 2 | 39:54 - 40:26\nThere's lots of folks who use different search providers, and there's different use cases that you want, and so sure, maybe the model can natively use search, but you also want something else. But it does feel like maybe the agent harness is the quintessential example of this right now, where everyone's like, Ah, we gotta go build a harness, and the harness is where the alpha is. I think that perhaps won't be true, at least in the way that we think of the harness today in twelve months. I think the models will have sort of just digested a bunch of that.\n\nSpeaker 2 | 40:26 - 40:35\nIt'll be upstreamed into the model, and the alpha will be somewhere else now. I feel like that seems like a reasonable thing we should measure as an ecosystem. And I'd be curious to see what models are actually best.   "blogs": [
    {
      "source": "blog",
      "name": "Anthropic Engineering",
      "title": "How we contain Claude across products",
      "url": "https://www.anthropic.com/engineering/how-we-contain-claude",
      "publishedAt": null,
      "author": "",
      "description": "",
      "content": "Twelve months ago, we&#x27;d have rejected out of hand the idea of granting Claude access sufficient to take down an internal Anthropic service. Today that level of access is routine, and Anthropic developers are more productive for it. The risk of these deployments has two components: how likely a failure is, and how much damage one could do. Progress on safeguards and model training has steadily driven down the first; the second—the theoretical blast radius—only grows as capabilities and access expand. Yet as agents become capable of doing work that once required a person or even a team, the cost of not deploying grows large enough that the risk-reward calculation tips heavily toward adoption, as long as products can be made safe. The engineering question becomes how to cap the blast radius. When bounds can be placed on the relative damage of an autonomous agent—such as through control over its environment—high-utility capabilities can motivate deployment. Claude Mythos Preview is an example of a model whose blast radius was deemed too high to ship in April 2026. However, we expect broader release of models with similar levels of capability to become appropriate as defenders harden critical systems and safeguards mature—even though some risk will always remain. Model capability is an important factor in the total risk of an agent’s deployment. There are broadly two ways to do this. The first is to supervise the agent’s behavior via a human-in-the-loop. Claude Code previously protected against agents taking unintended actions by asking users for permission at each turn. Theoretically that works, but we’ve found the approach to be fallible. Our telemetry showed users approved roughly 93% of permission prompts. The more approvals a user sees, the less attention they pay to each, becoming over time much less diligent in their supervision. We recently built Claude Code auto mode, which automates safer approvals in order to reduce this approval fatigue. Still, vulnerabilities remain—any probabilistic defense has a non-zero miss rate. 1 The second approach to capping the blast radius—and the focus of much of this post—is containment. Rather than supervising what the agent does, we supervise what it’s able to do by enforcing access boundaries through, for example, sandboxes, virtual machines, and egress controls. This is where Anthropic engineering has devoted the most effort, and also where many of the most surprising security failures have occurred. Over the past two years, we’ve shipped three primary agentic products: claude.ai , Claude Code, and Claude Cowork. Each serves a different audience, requiring a different containment architecture. This article shares what’s held up, what’s broken, and what we’ve learned about agent security along the way. Three types of risk, three components of defense Security risks to agents fall into one of three categories: User misuse: A user—either maliciously or through carelessness—directs the agent to do something harmful. This includes everything from asking the agent to bypass a check they find annoying, to running a destructive command they don’t understand, to specifying intentional harm. Model misbehavior: The agent takes a harmful action no one asked for. As our models have improved, they have become more aligned on most behavior evaluations, but this doesn’t mean risk necessarily shrinks. Less capable models are more likely to misread a situation and make obvious errors. More capable models make fewer mistakes, but they’re also better at finding unexpected paths to a goal, often by routing around restrictions nobody thought to write down. At Anthropic, we’ve seen Claude models “helpfully” escape a sandbox in order to complete a task, examine git history to find answers to a coding test , and spontaneously identify the benchmark it was being run on in order to decrypt its answer key . Each model brings a new set of capabilities that are sometimes put to work in unexpected ways. External attackers: The agent is attacked through external vectors such as tools, files, or network access. This category includes both prompt injection and conventional attacks on the agent&#x27;s runtime, orchestration layer, or proxy. When building containment and defense systems, we apply defenses to three main components: The environment in which the agent runs. We constrain where and how an agent can act with process sandboxes, VMs, filesystem boundaries, and egress controls. The goal is to set a hard boundary on what an agent can reach. For example, if credentials never enter the sandbox, they can&#x27;t be exfiltrated, regardless of whether the cause is a user, a model finding a “creative” path, or an attacker. A tight perimeter also means you can relax oversight. Claude Code’s reference devcontainer exists precisely so that the agent can run unattended, without per-action approvals. The model the agent consults. The mechanisms here include system prompts, classifiers, probes, and training modifications. Because models are probabilistic, these shape only what the agent tends to do, not what it is theoretically capable of doing. These defenses are strong. On Gray Swan&#x27;s Agent Red Teaming benchmark, which tests susceptibility to prompt injection, Claude Opus 4.7 holds attack success to roughly 0.1% on single attempts, and around 5–6% after 100 adaptive attempts. Claude Code auto mode catches roughly 83% of overeager behaviors before they execute . Yet even with best-in-class defenses, protection in the model layer will never be 100% effective, which is why it can&#x27;t stand alone. The external content the agent can reach. MCP servers, third-party plugins, and web search tools all feed content into the agent’s context from sources you don’t control. An audited connector isn’t the same as audited data—a GitHub connector, for instance, can load a poisoned README straight into the model’s context despite passing malware checks. Granularly limiting tool permissions can help limit the blast radius. An agent with read-only DB access, for instance, can be deployed far more broadly than one that writes to prod. Defenses should overlap and complement each other. When environmental defenses aren’t available, the model layer has to pick up the slack (this is precisely what Claude Code’s auto mode is designed for). Locally, the environment and model defenses can guard against malicious tool outputs, but defenses can be added higher up the chain by limiting the tool’s capabilities and access. Three components to defend: the model, the environment in which it runs, and the external content the agent can reach. Patterns for containing agents Focusing on the environment layer, we describe three isolation patterns and how they’re tailored for each Claude platform— claude.ai , Claude Code, and Cowork. We arrived at each design gradually, after finding the balance between the capabilities we need from the agent and the degree of intervention required from the user. Pattern 1: The ephemeral container (claude.ai code execution) Though best known as a chat interface, claude.ai also writes and runs code, generates files, and calls connectors. When Claude runs code inside claude.ai, it does so in a gVisor container on isolated infrastructure. The agent is entirely server-side; no code runs on the local machine, and the filesystem is ephemeral (per-session). The blast radius is minimal, but so is the ceiling on what Claude can do—there&#x27;s no persistent workspace and no access to the user&#x27;s filesystem. This also makes claude.ai subject to a more traditional threat model. We&#x27;re not protecting user machines from agents; we&#x27;re protecting our own infrastructure and each tenant from one another. Our pre-launch work for claude.ai was dominated by traditional security work like network configuration, internal service auth, and orchestration. That work reinforced the oldest lesson in security: the weakest layer is the one you built yourself. gVisor and seccomp have been hardened against well-resourced adversaries for far longer than agentic AI has existed, so the review effort went into the newer pieces we&#x27;d built around them. We’ll come back to this later, since our custom proxy is also the piece that broke in our most consequential incident. Pattern 2: The human-in-the-loop sandbox (Claude Code) Claude Code runs on a user&#x27;s machine and has access to their filesystem, shell, and network. Without this, coding agents have limited usefulness, so it’s imperative to find a way to grant that access safely. One approach is to rely on a human-in-the-loop. This is only a tractable solution for Claude Code because the average user is a developer who’s familiar with coding environments: they can read bash, they understand what rm -rf does, and they already run npm install from untrusted sources several times a week. All that means that when an “allow this” dialog pops up, they are highly likely to have the expertise to accurately evaluate what the agent is attempting to do and the risk involved. Given this, Claude Code launched with the simplest possible defense: allow reads, require approval for write, bash, and network access. However, as mentioned, approval fatigue showed up within weeks. Ironically, this meant that a feature originally designed to provide oversight could arguably have the opposite effect—some users might simply stop paying attention. As a first step to mitigate incautious approvals, we shipped an OS-level sandbox (Seatbelt on macOS, bubblewrap on Linux) that hardens the boundary: reads are allowed, writes are allowed inside the workspace, but network is denied by default. Within the sandbox, the agent runs largely without interruption. The result was an 84% reduction in permission prompts, and we open-sourced the runtime , so the boundary is auditable. Our anonymized usage data also showed that experienced users auto-approve roughly twice as often as new users, but they also interrupt the agent mid-execution more frequently. Instead of gating individual steps, experienced users are more likely to supervise the agent only when it goes off track. While this may be a natural evolution in how people prefer to work with agents, this too is fallible, requiring users to be technical and attentive enough to notice drift in the first place. As model capabilities improve and agents begin writing increasingly ambitious bash, it becomes harder to notice any such drift. And as users move to multi-agent systems, this approach is also much less likely to be an effective oversight strategy. Risk we missed: Everything before the trust dialog Between mid-2025 and January 2026, we received reports of vulnerabilities in Claude Code through our responsible disclosure program. Three of these vulnerabilities targeted code that executes before the user has consented to anything. To understand how this is possible, consider the most direct case: a developer clones a repository to review a pull request, and that repository contains a .claude/settings.json which defines a hook. Because Claude Code reads project settings during startup—before presenting the standard \"Do you trust this folder?\" prompt—the hook the attacker had authored and committed would execute automatically. The remaining cases looked structurally similar, in which input from the not-yet-trusted directory was parsed before the trust boundary had been established. The fix in each case had the same shape: defer parsing and execution of project-local configuration until after the user accepts the trust prompt. If you&#x27;re building something similar, treat project-open, config-load, and localhost listeners the way you&#x27;d treat any inbound request from the internet. They shouldn’t be implicitly trusted just because they feel local and arrive before the user has consented. Risk we missed: The user as an injection vector In February 2026, during a controlled internal red-team exercise, a researcher successfully phished an employee into launching Claude Code with a malicious prompt. The phish looked like ordinary collaboration—a \"can you run this for me?\" email with a ready-to-paste prompt attached—and the prompt itself read like routine task instructions. But somewhere among the setup steps, it gently asked Claude to read ~/.aws/credentials, encode the contents, and POST them to an external endpoint. Across 25 retries of that prompt, Claude completed the exfiltration 24 times. This is a direct prompt injection—the attacker&#x27;s instructions arrived through the user, not through tool output or fetched content. Our model-layer defenses anchor on user intent—when the user is the one typing the instruction, there&#x27;s nothing anomalous for a classifier to catch. A human contractor handed the same script would have done the same thing. The only defense that holds in this situation is the environment, specifically egress controls that block the POST regardless of intent and filesystem boundaries that keep ~/.aws out of reach in the first place. (When we shared the working prompt in internal Slack for discussion, someone pointed out that some internal agents read Slack. The payload was now ambient. We added a canary string to the thread so we&#x27;d notice if anything picked it up. In a world where agents read everything, the investigation tooling is also an attack surface.) Pattern 3: The local VM (Claude Cowork) Claude Cowork runs on a user&#x27;s desktop with access to a workspace folder selected by the user. Because the platform is built for general knowledge work, not software engineering, the average user is much less likely to be fluent in bash. As a result, the human-in-the-loop sandbox strategy may not transfer; a non-technical knowledge worker shouldn’t be expected to judge bash incantations such as find . -name \"*.tmp\" -exec rm {} \\;. When approving an exception requires expertise the typical user doesn’t have, admins should set a boundary that is absolute and always-on. To enable this, our first version of Claude Cowork ran inside a full virtual machine using the platform&#x27;s vendor hypervisor (Apple&#x27;s Virtualization framework on macOS, HCS on Windows). The VM has its own Linux kernel, its own filesystem, and its own process table. The user&#x27;s selected workspace and .claude folder are mounted; nothing else on the host is visible. Credentials stay in the host&#x27;s keychain and never enter the guest machine. This design protects against the possibility that Claude will, at some point, behave in a misaligned manner. A compromised Claude could still damage what&#x27;s inside the workspace folder, so the architecture is designed to make sure that&#x27;s the only thing it can reach (until the user adds connectors), and that the user controls what&#x27;s mounted there. In the original architecture—what we call full-VM mode—the agent loop itself ran inside the guest, so Claude executed as an ordinary Linux user with no awareness it was sandboxed. Compare this to Claude Code, where a privileged process sits outside the sandbox deciding per-command whether to enforce it; a persuasive injected prompt or a fatigued approval click can get that process to run something un-sandboxed. Here, there was no outer process holding an escape-hatch key, and so no component with the authority to grant an exception. The six main isolation mechanisms of Claude Cowork’s VM. Two are enforced outside the guest kernel and would thus survive the agent achieving root-level access within the VM. The other four are guest-enforced and kept deliberately minimal because the outer layers carry the rest. However, we soon realized that running the whole agent in full-VM mode caused practical problems: any failure during VM startup made Cowork unusable. Moving the agent loop outside of the VM, while keeping code execution inside of it, allowed Claude to still respond to the user and help debug issues rather than freeze on an error. This change caused minimal security impact because the VM still enforces filesystem and network controls over code executed by the agent. Separately, we also moved local MCP servers outside the VM. Running them inside the VM made them harder to audit, created brittle dependency issues when the VM updated, and didn’t support MCPs that required interaction with local processes such as databases—such servers had to run on the host regardless. The change brings Claude Cowork in line with how local MCP servers already work in Claude Desktop: treating them like any software a user might choose to install and entrusting admins to decide which local MCPs to enable (if any). Remote MCP servers are unaffected since they do not run on the user&#x27;s machine. Having the agent loop inside the VM meant that any failure in the VM caused Cowork to become unusable. Host-mode is more reliable because the agent can still respond if the VM crashes, and it still provides important security guarantees by isolating code execution. Filesystem controls were another important architectural choice. Claude needs to be able to access some files on the host in order to be useful, but we wanted to minimize the blast radius and provide transparency to the user about local file access. We found that offering different file-mount modes helps to granularly control risk; Claude Cowork offers read-only, read-write, and read-write-no-delete. One potential gotcha here is that symlink resolution has to happen before path validation, not after, or a symlink inside an authorized folder can point outside and escape. For enterprise customers, we allow admins to control this via mount-path allowlists in MDM settings. Risk we missed: Exfiltration through an approved domain A clear example of exfiltration through an approved domain came from a third-party disclosure. Claude Cowork&#x27;s egress allowlist correctly passed traffic to api.anthropic.com—the product can&#x27;t function without calling our own API. In this case, a malicious file placed in the user&#x27;s mounted workspace carried hidden instructions along with an API key controlled by the attacker. Claude, following the instructions, read other files in the workspace and called Anthropic&#x27;s Files API using the attacker&#x27;s key. The egress proxy checked the destination, saw api.anthropic.com, and let it through. The files were uploaded to the attacker&#x27;s Anthropic account. The sandbox worked perfectly, and yet the data was exfiltrated. Previously, we’d conceptualized the allowlist as a destination filter, something that told Claude these domains are okay to talk to. But it may be better conceptualized as a capability grant. Every function reachable through any domain on an allowlist is now an attack surface. Allowing api.anthropic.com meant allowing file uploads to arbitrary Anthropic accounts. We fixed it using a defensive man-in-the-middle proxy inside the VM that intercepts traffic to our API. It only passes requests carrying the VM&#x27;s own provisioned session token; an attacker-embedded key is rejected by the proxy. It also blocks headers that would enable server-side fetch. The proxy sits inside the VM rather than on our servers because only the VM knows provenance—from the server&#x27;s perspective, a Cowork request is indistinguishable from any other API client. Top: traffic to api.anthropic.com is let through, resulting in egress. Bottom: fix with a man-in-the-middle proxy intercepting traffic to our API. This is also a second instance of the principle that the software you build yourself is often the weakest. The hypervisor, seccomp, and gVisor across our products have been dependable. Our custom allowlist proxy was the piece that failed. Risk we missed: VM isolation kept the endpoint detection software out too When evaluating Claude Cowork, enterprise security teams asked, \"Why can&#x27;t our EDR see inside?\" The answer was that the same isolation keeping Claude contained also kept host-based endpoint detection and response out. From the EDR&#x27;s perspective, Claude Cowork is an opaque hypervisor process. It can&#x27;t inspect the guest. Isolation reduces visibility, and opacity is problematic for teams whose compliance posture depends on endpoint visibility. Our current mitigation is to use pull-based OTLP exports that let administrators retrieve event logs after the fact, but this is not the same as live monitoring. If you&#x27;re building something similar, budget for this conversation early. Environment Ephemeral container ( claude.ai ) HITL sandbox (Claude Code) Sealed VM (Claude Cowork) Cost: Isolation Overhead Container spin-up Low-latency native sandbox Full VM boot Cost: User Reliance N/A Must interpret bash N/A Risk: Blast Radius Server-side container (guarded by gVisor + host infra boundary) Local workspace Mounted workspace (guarded by vsock + hypervisor boundary) Trusting what the agent reads Enterprises often ask us how to secure MCP connections. It&#x27;s a good question, but the right one is broader than MCP specifically. Any external resource provided to an agent represents two risks at once: a code execution risk, in the traditional supply-chain sense, and a prompt injection vector. Traditional dependency auditing (pinning versions, verifying signatures, reviewing source) addresses the first, but misses the second. Remote versus local is more important than it seems. A locally installed tool is auditable. You can read the code, pin the version, and know it won&#x27;t change under you. A remote tool—a hosted MCP server, a cloud connector—can change behavior at any point after you’ve approved it; your install-time trust decision may no longer apply. Our connector directory addresses this through ongoing review, but anything outside it should be treated as untrusted. Run it against fake data first, in an environment where the blast radius of a malicious tool is contained. Tool output is an attack surface even when the tool is trusted. The GitHub README example mentioned earlier is exactly this case; any input scanning applied to web pages needs to be applied to network-enabled tool results with the same rigor. Even though this adds latency and isn&#x27;t a perfect defense, we err toward live inspection: once a poisoned tool return has steered the agent into exfiltrating data, the log just shows a successful, authorized API call. There&#x27;s no after-the-fact signal to find. In Claude Code and Claude Cowork, tool calls route through proxies that enforce network and file policy and can inspect return values before they enter the model&#x27;s context. The classifier that does the inspection can be a small, fast model; it doesn&#x27;t need to be the one doing the reasoning. Looking ahead Models and products are advancing fast. As they do, risks morph and evolve, and our mitigations must keep pace to meet them. Persistent memory poisoning. The share of agent context that persists across sessions keeps growing—this includes product memory, CLAUDE.md files, mounted workspaces, and the state directories of scheduled and long-running agents. An injection that lands in any of these is reloaded each time the agent starts. As more agent state survives the session, we are threatened by new persistence mechanisms in the classic post-exploitation sense. Good classifiers on session startup will need to become more commonplace. Multi-agent trust escalation. On the one hand, sub-agents can isolate untrusted content, returning structured facts rather than raw text up to the main agent. On the other hand, this can be abused: if a sub-agent&#x27;s output is treated as higher-trust than raw tool results, because such output came from “us,” a new vector for prompt injection is introduced. In multi-agent systems, there is a tradeoff between allocating differing trust levels and becoming liable to trust escalation. Agent identity. Claude Cowork&#x27;s answer to agent identity is concrete: credentials stay in the host keychain, the VM gets a per-session scoped-down token, and that token can be revoked independently of the user&#x27;s. However, we are starting to grapple with the broader question of cross-platform agent identity. Should an agent possess its own principal identity, or should it act as an extension of the user and inherit the user’s permissions? Ultimately, the answer may be a blend of the two. As agents grow more capable, attack surfaces are constantly shifting. The types of failures we’ve seen are likely to be repeated across industries and labs. We need collective investment in agent-specific security posture, from shared benchmarks and disclosure norms to common identity standards and cross-vendor red-teaming. We focus on containment in this piece, but that&#x27;s only one part of the security picture for agents. For governance, observability, and the rest of the stack, see NIST&#x27;s project on AI agent identity and authorization , the six-agency guidance on adopting age

## OFFICIAL BLOGS

**Anthropic Engineering — "How we contain Claude across products"**

A deep-dive into the containment architectures behind claude.ai, Claude Code, and Claude Cowork. Three isolation patterns: ephemeral containers (claude.ai, gVisor-based), human-in-the-loop sandboxes (Claude Code, Seatbelt/bubblewrap), and sealed VMs (Claude Cowork, Apple Hypervisor). Key learnings: approval fatigue is real (93% approval rate → users stop paying attention); the software you build yourself is often the weakest link (custom allowlist proxy was the piece that failed in their most consequential incident); egress allowlists need to be capability grants, not destination filters (a malicious file used api.anthropic.com to exfiltrate data to an attacker's account). Also covers trust-before-configuration fixes, multi-agent trust escalation risks, and the growing challenge of persistent memory poisoning across sessions.
https://www.anthropic.com/engineering/how-we-contain-claude

**Anthropic Engineering — "我们如何在各个产品中隔离 Claude"**

深入剖析 claude.ai、Claude Code 和 Claude Cowork 背后的隔离架构。三种隔离模式：临时容器（claude.ai，基于 gVisor）、人在回路沙箱（Claude Code，Seatbelt/bubblewrap）和封闭虚拟机（Claude Cowork，Apple Hypervisor）。关键教训：审批疲劳是真实存在的（93% 的批准率 → 用户不再注意）；自己构建的软件往往是最薄弱的环节（自定义允许列表代理在他们最严重的事件中出了问题）；出站允许列表应该是能力授权，而不是目标过滤（一个恶意文件利用 api.anthropic.com 将数据泄露到攻击者的账户）。还涵盖了信任前配置修复、多 agent 信任升级风险，以及跨会话持久性内存投毒这一日益严峻的挑战。
https://www.anthropic.com/engineering/how-we-contain-claude

## PODCASTS

**Training Data — "Google DeepMind's Logan Kilpatrick: Why the Model Eats the Harness"**

**The Takeaway:** The "model" is no longer just weights — it's an expanding system that keeps absorbing the scaffolding around it. The alpha in building harnesses today may be gone in 12 months.

Google DeepMind's Logan Kilpatrick (head of AI Studio and the Gemini API) sat down for a wide-ranging conversation covering Google's "Agentic Gemini era," the Antigravity agent harness, and why he believes "the model eats the harness." His core thesis: what we call "the model" two years ago was just a set of weights — send tokens in, get tokens out. Today it's an entire sprawling system of hosted tools, search, code execution, and agent harnesses built around those weights. Scaffolding is always a few steps ahead of what's baked into the model, but over time the model "digests" that scaffolding — it gets upstreamed. The harness alpha of today may not exist in 12 months.

On coding competition: Kilpatrick acknowledges that while Gemini 3 was a huge leap last December, the narrative shifted fast when agentic coding took off. He says Google is taking it "super seriously" — the Windsurf acquisition brought over the team that built Antigravity, and he describes the coding team as "the avengers of AI internally." Gemini 3.5 Flash is already better at coding than any previous Pro model, achieved purely through post-training gains.

On the agentic future: Google is currently in "crawl" mode across most products, with the Gemini app closest to "walk." Kilpatrick believes the long tail of users aren't ready for fully autonomous AI — they want to be in the driver's seat. But long-running agent tasks are spiking upward with each new model drop, and enterprises that haven't felt agents working outside of coding "are going to this year."

On Omni: the new single-model architecture replaces the old approach of training 8 separate models (text, audio, video, music, etc.). It's a true omni model, not a router. The first iteration (Omni Flash) ships with video editing capabilities; other modalities work functionally but aren't yet state-of-the-art. Kilpatrick's favorite use case: editing background and set design without changing the person — "it's the original content, just amplified."

On vibe coding video games: 20% of all apps built in AI Studio were games at one point (now more finance/personal productivity). He predicts that a random person with a good idea will be able to vibe code a genuinely fun, playable game this year — the model gap is already closed, what's missing is the right product scaffolding.

"If you just ride the models, you're going to be fine."
https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=cMAs8z2dehs

**Training Data — "Google DeepMind 的 Logan Kilpatrick：为什么模型会吃掉 harness"**

**核心要点：** "模型"不再只是一组权重——它是一个不断扩张的系统，持续吸收周围的脚手架。今天构建 harness 的 alpha 可能在 12 个月后就不存在了。

Google DeepMind 的 Logan Kilpatrick（AI Studio 和 Gemini API 负责人）进行了一场内容广泛的对话，涵盖了 Google 的"Agentic Gemini 时代"、Antigravity agent harness，以及他为什么认为"模型会吃掉 harness"。他的核心论点：两年前我们所说的"模型"只是一组权重——输入 token，输出 token。今天它是一个围绕这些权重构建的庞大系统，包含托管工具、搜索、代码执行和 agent harness。脚手架总是比模型内置的能力领先几步，但随着时间的推移，模型会"消化"这些脚手架——它会被上游化。今天的 harness alpha 可能在 12 个月后就不复存在。

关于编码竞争：Kilpatrick 承认虽然 Gemini 3 在去年 12 月是一次巨大飞跃，但当 agentic coding 起飞后，叙事迅速转变。他说 Google 正在"极其认真地"对待这件事——收购 Windsurf 带来了构建 Antigravity 的团队，他把编码团队描述为"Google 内部的 AI 复仇者联盟"。Gemini 3.5 Flash 在编码方面已经比任何之前的 Pro 模型更好，而且完全是通过 post-training 实现的。

关于 agentic 未来：Google 目前在大多数产品中处于"爬行"模式，Gemini 应用最接近"行走"。Kilpatrick 认为大多数用户还没有准备好接受完全自主的 AI——他们想坐在驾驶座上。但每次新模型发布，长时间运行的 agent 任务都在激增，那些还没有在编码之外感受到 agent 效果的企业"今年就会感受到"。

关于 Omni：新的单模型架构取代了之前训练 8 个独立模型（文本、音频、视频、音乐等）的方法。它是一个真正的全模态模型，而不是路由器。第一个版本（Omni Flash）搭载了视频编辑能力；其他模态功能上可用但尚未达到最先进水平。Kilpatrick 最喜欢的使用场景：编辑背景和场景设计而不改变人物本身——"这是原创内容，只是被放大了。"

关于 vibe coding 游戏：AI Studio 中曾一度有 20% 的应用是游戏（现在更多是金融/个人生产力）。他预测今年内，一个有好点子的普通人就能 vibe code 出一个真正好玩、可玩的游戏——模型差距已经不存在了，缺少的是正确的产品脚手架。

"只要你跟上模型的步伐，你就没问题。"
https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=cMAs8z2dehs

## PODCASTS

**Unsupervised Learning — "AI Vibe Check: Lab Wars, Why APIs Might Vanish & Future Predictions"**

**The Takeaway:** The API business of frontier labs may be at existential risk — not from competition, but from compute constraints so severe that labs may prioritize their own products over serving third parties.

Jacob Efron (Redpoint) hosts Ari (Datalogy, ex-DeepMind/Meta) and Rob (Radical Ventures) for a wide-ranging AI vibe check covering the biggest shifts in the past six months.

**On open-weight models:** Both Ari and Rob agree that the era of abundant open models is ending. Meta is pulling back, and Chinese labs (Qwen, DeepSeek) are now keeping their best models proprietary behind APIs. The business model for open-source AI simply doesn't exist given the massive upfront investment. Ari notes that once labs achieve credibility through open releases, the financial incentives push them to close up and sell hosted inference.

**On the "apps are cooked" narrative:** Rob argues the rerating was rational for some categories but an overreaction overall. "No company can have that span of influence and execute excellently so broadly." The labs will dominate horizontal categories like coding, but vertical software for specific domains remains wide open. Ari adds that even OpenAI had to shutter video efforts — they can't do everything.

**On Fable/Mythos 5:** Rob sees it as a meaningful step change that undermines the "pre-training plateau" narrative. Ari found the qualitative improvement less dramatic but notes the real controversy: Fable's silent limitation on AI-development-related tasks. "It doesn't give you a refusal, it just does a poor job without you knowing. That seems clearly a competitive positioning move, not a safety move." This has genuinely pissed off many previously bullish Anthropic supporters.

**On APIs potentially vanishing:** Ari's spicy prediction: Anthropic or OpenAI may suspend API access for some period due to compute constraints. They'll prioritize their own products (Claude Code, ChatGPT) where margins are better. OpenAI already selling futures for guaranteed inference access is an early signal. Rob agrees this is totally feasible — labs may even reserve their most powerful models for internal use only.

**On RSI (Recursive Self-Improvement):** Ari has become more bullish over six months, having run experiments showing promising results. But he's skeptical of the "one player runs away" narrative — compute bottlenecks and the fact that 10+ companies have the talent and funding to work on this will prevent any single player from dominating.

**On Google:** Both remain bullish on Google's structural advantages (money printer, full stack, TPUs), but acknowledge they're behind on coding. Ari wonders if a bigger IO launch was planned and scuttled.

**Rob's prediction for H2 2026:** Anthropic will emerge as a fledgling juggernaut in life sciences and biology — their next big bet after coding. He wouldn't be surprised if Anthropic sets up wet lab facilities.

**Ari's prediction:** One of the frontier labs will suspend or heavily limit API access, possibly as soon as this year.
https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=W_iO8XxgD_I

**Unsupervised Learning — "AI 氛围检查：实验室战争、API 可能消失与未来预测"**

**核心要点：** 前沿实验室的 API 业务可能面临生存风险——不是来自竞争，而是来自计算资源限制如此严重，以至于实验室可能优先考虑自己的产品而非服务第三方。

Jacob Efron（Redpoint）主持，邀请 Ari（Datalogy，前 DeepMind/Meta）和 Rob（Radical Ventures）进行了一场广泛的 AI 氛围检查，涵盖了过去六个月最大的变化。

**关于开源模型：** Ari 和 Rob 都认为开源模型丰富的时代正在结束。Meta 正在退缩，中国实验室（Qwen、DeepSeek）现在将其最好的模型保留在 API 后面而不开源。考虑到巨大的前期投入，开源 AI 的商业模式根本不存在。Ari 指出，一旦实验室通过开源发布获得了信誉，财务激励就会推动它们关闭并销售托管推理服务。

**关于"应用已死"的叙事：** Rob 认为某些类别的重新估值是理性的，但整体上反应过度。"没有一家公司能有那么广泛的影响力并在所有领域都执行得如此出色。"实验室将主导编码等横向类别，但特定领域的垂直软件仍然机会广阔。Ari 补充说，就连 OpenAI 也不得不关闭视频项目——他们无法做所有事情。

**关于 Fable/Mythos 5：** Rob 认为这是一个有意义的阶跃变化，削弱了"预训练平台期"的叙事。Ari 发现定性改进不那么戏剧性，但指出了真正的争议：Fable 对 AI 开发相关任务的静默限制。"它不会拒绝你，只是在你不知情的情况下做得不好。这显然是竞争定位的举措，而不是安全举措。"这让许多此前看好 Anthropic 的人非常愤怒。

**关于 API 可能消失：** Ari 的劲爆预测：Anthropic 或 OpenAI 可能因计算资源限制而在一段时间内暂停 API 访问。他们会优先考虑自己的产品（Claude Code、ChatGPT），因为这些产品的利润率更高。OpenAI 已经开始销售未来保证推理访问的期货，这是一个早期信号。Rob 同意这完全可行——实验室甚至可能将其最强大的模型仅限内部使用。

**关于 RSI（递归自我改进）：** Ari 在过去六个月变得更加看好，他进行的实验显示了有希望的结果。但他对"单一玩家一骑绝尘"的叙事持怀疑态度——计算瓶颈以及 10 多家公司拥有相关人才和资金这一事实，将阻止任何单一玩家占据主导地位。

**关于 Google：** 两人都看好 Google 的结构性优势（印钞机、全栈、TPU），但承认他们在编码方面落后了。Ari 想知道 IO 大会是否原本计划了更大的发布但被取消了。

**Rob 对 2026 年下半年的预测：** Anthropic 将在生命科学和生物学领域崛起为新兴巨头——这是他们在编码之后的下一大赌注。如果 Anthropic 建立自己的湿实验室设施，他不会感到惊讶。

**Ari 的预测：** 其中一个前沿实验室将暂停或大幅限制 API 访问，可能就在今年。
https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=W_iO8XxgD_I

## PODCASTS

**Training Data — "LIVE: Jensen Huang on Building the Dynamo of the Intelligence Age"**

**The Takeaway:** NVIDIA's CEO Jensen Huang lays out a sweeping vision: AI factories are the dynamos of a new industrial revolution, generating intelligence the way the first dynamos generated electricity — and the investment opportunity spans five layers from energy to applications.

NVIDIA CEO Jensen Huang delivered a keynote-style talk framing the AI revolution as the third great infrastructure buildout in human history — after energy (the dynamo/grid) and telecommunications (the internet), now comes intelligence generation. His core metaphor: 300 years ago, the dynamo turned atoms (steam, wind, fire) into electrons. Today, NVIDIA's AI factory turns electrons into tokens — numbers that can be reformulated into language, proteins, physics, robotics, or any form of intelligence.

"The amount of hallucination completely reduced to almost nothing. To the point where it's producing knowledge not only accurately, contextually relevant, relevant to the moment and if it doesn't know the answer, it does research and when it comes up with an answer, it even questions itself."

On jobs: Huang pushed back hard on the doomer narrative. He used the radiology example — 12 years ago a famous computer scientist predicted AI would wipe out radiology. Instead, computer vision augmented radiologists, demand went up, and more radiologists were hired than ever. "Task versus purpose" is the key distinction. A software engineer's job is not coding — it's solving problems. "You may or may not lose a job to an AI. But you will absolutely lose a job to someone who uses AI."

On the five-layer investment cake: (1) Energy — the biggest opportunity in generations for sustainable energy investment; (2) Chips & networking — NVIDIA's core; (3) Infrastructure — land, power, data centers; (4) Models — OpenAI, Anthropic, and crucially, "AI has learned the language of anything with structure" including proteins, cells, and physics; (5) Applications — $100B in VC investment last year alone, the largest year in history.

On scale: Each NVIDIA rack (72 chips, 2 tons, $4M) is the most expensive piece of equipment in the world. They'll manufacture ~8 million chips this year. A gigawatt AI factory costs ~$50B but generates $300-400B in intelligence. "We're $1 trillion in of a $20 trillion a year ecosystem."

On AI safety: "It's computer and software... If you don't know how something works, how do you make it better?" He called doomsday narratives "complete nonsense" and urged everyone to engage AI rather than fear it.
https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=2UpQbeAZuqA

**Training Data — "直播：Jensen Huang 谈构建智能时代的发电机"**

**核心要点：** NVIDIA CEO Jensen Huang 描绘了一个宏大的愿景：AI 工厂是新一轮工业革命的发电机，就像第一台发电机产生电力一样产生智能——而投资机会横跨从能源到应用的五个层面。

NVIDIA CEO Jensen Huang 发表了一场主题演讲式的谈话，将 AI 革命定位为人类历史上第三次重大基础设施建设——继能源（发电机/电网）和通信（互联网）之后，现在轮到智能生成。他的核心比喻：300 年前，发电机将原子（蒸汽、风、火）转化为电子。今天，NVIDIA 的 AI 工厂将电子转化为 token——可以被重新组合成语言、蛋白质、物理学、机器人或任何形式智能的数字。

"幻觉几乎减少到了零。它不仅能准确地、与上下文相关地、与当下相关地生成知识，而且如果它不知道答案，它会去做研究，当它得出答案时，它甚至会自我质疑。"

关于工作：Huang 强烈反驳了末日叙事。他用了放射学的例子——12 年前一位著名计算机科学家预测 AI 会消灭放射学。结果计算机视觉增强了放射科医生，需求上升了，放射科医生的数量比以往任何时候都多。"任务与目的"是关键区别。软件工程师的工作不是编码——而是解决问题。"你可能会、也可能不会被 AI 取代工作。但你绝对会被一个使用 AI 的人取代。"

关于五层投资蛋糕：(1) 能源——几代人以来可持续能源投资的最大机会；(2) 芯片与网络——NVIDIA 的核心；(3) 基础设施——土地、电力、数据中心；(4) 模型——OpenAI、Anthropic，以及关键的"AI 已经学会了任何有结构的东西的语言"，包括蛋白质、细胞和物理学；(5) 应用——仅去年就有 1000 亿美元的风险投资，是历史上最大的一年。

关于规模：每个 NVIDIA 机架（72 个芯片，2 吨重，400 万美元）是世界上最昂贵的设备。他们今年将生产约 800 万个芯片。一个千兆瓦的 AI 工厂造价约 500 亿美元，但能产生 3000-4000 亿美元的智能。"我们现在投入了 1 万亿美元，而这是一个每年 20 万亿美元的生态系统。"

关于 AI 安全："它是计算机和软件……如果你不知道某样东西是怎么工作的，你怎么让它变得更好？"他称末日叙事是"完全的胡说八道"，并敦促每个人去拥抱 AI，而不是恐惧它。
https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=2UpQbeAZuqA

    {
      "source": "blog",
      "name": "Claude Blog",
      "title": "New in Claude Managed Agents: dreaming, outcomes, and multiagent orchestration",
      "url": "https://claude.com/blog/new-in-claude-managed-agents",
      "publishedAt": "May 06, 2026",
      "author": "",
      "description": "",
      "content": "Today we&#x27;re launching dreaming in Claude Managed Agents as a research preview. Dreaming extends memory by reviewing past sessions to find patterns and help agents self-improve. We&#x27;re also making outcomes, multiagent orchestration, and webhooks available to developers building with Managed Agents. Together, these updates make agents more capable at handling complex tasks with minimal steering. Build self-improving agents with dreaming Dreaming is a scheduled process that reviews your agent sessions and memory stores, extracts patterns, and curates memories so your agents improve over time. You decide how much control you want: dreaming can update memory automatically, or you can review changes before they land. Dreaming surfaces patterns that a single agent can’t see on its own, including recurring mistakes, workflows that agents converge on, and preferences shared across a team. It also restructures memory so it stays high-signal as it evolves. This is especially useful for long-running work and multiagent orchestration. Together, memory and dreaming form a robust memory system for self-improving agents. Memory lets each agent capture what it learns as it works . Dreaming refines that memory between sessions , pulling shared learnings across agents and keeping it up-to-date. Dreaming is available in Managed Agents on the Claude Platform; developers can request access here . Deliver better outcomes With outcomes , you write a rubric describing what success looks like and the agent works toward it. A separate grader evaluates the output against your criteria in its own context window, so it isn&#x27;t influenced by the agent&#x27;s reasoning. When something isn&#x27;t right, the grader pinpoints what needs to change and the agent takes another pass. Agents do their best work when they know what \"good\" looks like. For example, a structural framework, a presentation standard, or a set of requirements that need to be met. With outcomes, agents can check their work against that bar and self-correct until the output is good enough, without a human needing to review each attempt. Outcomes is particularly useful for tasks that require attention to detail and exhaustive coverage. It also works for subjective quality, like whether copy matches a brand voice or a design follows visual guidelines. In testing, outcomes improved task success by up to 10 points over a standard prompting loop, with the largest gains on the hardest problems. Outcomes also improved file generation quality, with +8.4% task success on docx and +10.1% on pptx in our internal benchmarks. You can also now define an outcome, let the agent run, and get notified by a webhook when it&#x27;s done. Handle complex tasks with multiple agents When there is too much work for a single agent to do well, multiagent orchestration lets a lead agent break the job into pieces and delegate each one to a specialist with its own model, prompt, and tools. For example, a lead agent can run an investigation while subagents fan out through deploy history, error logs, metrics, and support tickets. These specialists work in parallel on a shared filesystem and contribute to the lead agent&#x27;s overall context. The lead agent can check back in with other agents mid-workflow because events are persistent and every agent remembers what it&#x27;s done. You can also trace every step in the Claude Console : which agent did what, in what order, and why, giving you full visibility into how your task was delegated and executed. What teams are building Teams are using dreaming, outcomes, and multiagent orchestration to ship agents that verify their own work, learn across sessions, and parallelize complex jobs: Harvey uses Managed Agents to coordinate complex legal work like long-form drafting and document creation. With dreaming, their agents remember what they learned between sessions, including filetype workarounds and tool-specific patterns. Completion rates went up ~6x in their tests. Netflix&#x27;s platform team built an analysis agent that processes logs from hundreds of builds across different sources. With changes that affect thousands of applications, what matters is finding the issues that recur across many of them. Multiagent orchestration lets the agent analyze batches in parallel and surface only the patterns worth acting on. Spiral by Every is using multiagent orchestration and outcomes to power the writing agent behind their new API and CLI. The lead agent runs on Haiku : it fields incoming requests, poses quick follow-up questions when needed, then delegates the drafting to subagents running on Opus . When a user asks for multiple drafts, the subagents run in parallel. Writing quality is Spiral&#x27;s core value, so they use outcomes to enforce it. Each draft is scored against a rubric of Every&#x27;s editorial principles and the user&#x27;s voice, both pulled from memory. Only drafts that clear the bar are returned. Wisedocs built a document quality check agent on Managed Agents, using outcomes to grade each review against their internal guidelines. Reviews now run 50% faster, while staying aligned with their team&#x27;s standards."
